(print)<br />748,459 (digital)<br />1.6 million (combined)&#32;(as of July\xe2\x80\x93December 2019<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>)</td></tr><tr><th rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://economist.com">economist<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>The Economist</b></i> is an international <a href="/wiki/Weekly_newspaper" title="Weekly newspaper">weekly newspaper</a> printed in <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding#Thermally_activated_binding" title="Bookbinding">magazine-format</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_publishing" title="Electronic publishing">published digitally</a> that focuses on current affairs, international business, politics, and technology. Based in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, England, the newspaper is owned by <a href="/wiki/Economist_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Economist Group">The Economist Group</a>, with core editorial offices in the United States, as well as across major cities in continental Europe, Asia, and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. In August 2015, <a href="/wiki/Pearson_plc" title="Pearson plc">Pearson</a> sold its 50 percent stake in the newspaper to the Italian <a href="/wiki/Agnelli_family" title="Agnelli family">Agnelli family</a>\'s investment company, <a href="/wiki/Exor_(company)" title="Exor (company)">Exor</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">\xc2\xa3</a>469 million (<a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>531 million) and the paper re-acquired the remaining shares for \xc2\xa3185 million ($206 million). In 2019, their average global print circulation was over 909,476, while combined with their digital presence, runs to over 1.6 million. Across their social media platforms, it reaches an audience of 35 million, as of 2016. The newspaper has a prominent focus on <a href="/wiki/Data_journalism" title="Data journalism">data journalism</a> and analysis over <a href="/wiki/News_media" title="News media">original reporting</a>, to both criticism and acclaim.\n</p><p>Founded in 1843, <i>The Economist</i> was first circulated by Scottish economist <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(businessman)" title="James Wilson (businessman)">James Wilson</a> to muster support for abolishing the British <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a> (1815\xe2\x80\x9346), a system of <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">import tariffs</a>. Over time, the newspaper\'s coverage expanded further into <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economy</a> and eventually began running articles on current events, finance, commerce, and British politics. Throughout the mid-to-late 20th century, it greatly expanded its layout and format, adding opinion columns, special reports, <a href="/wiki/Political_cartoon" title="Political cartoon">political cartoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_editor" title="Letter to the editor">reader letters</a>, cover stories, art critique, book reviews, and technology features. The paper is often recognizable by its <a href="/wiki/Fire_engine_red" title="Fire engine red">fire engine red</a> nameplate and illustrated, topical covers. Individual articles are written anonymously, with no <a href="/wiki/Byline" title="Byline">byline</a>, in order for the paper to speak as one collective voice. The paper is supplemented by its sister lifestyle magazine, <i><a href="/wiki/1843_(magazine)" title="1843 (magazine)">1843</a></i>, and a variety of podcasts, films, and books.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/The_Economist_editorial_stance" title="The Economist editorial stance">editorial stance of <i>The Economist</i></a> primarily revolves around <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">social</a>, and most notably, <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>. Since its founding, it has supported <a href="/wiki/Radical_centrism" title="Radical centrism">radical centrism</a>, favouring policies and governments that maintain <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrist politics</a>. The newspaper typically champions Economic Liberalism, particularly <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_immigration" class="mw-redirect" title="Free immigration">free immigration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalisation</a>. Despite a pronounced editorial stance, it is seen as having little reporting bias, rigorous fact checking and strict copy editing.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Its extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Word_play" title="Word play">word play</a>, high subscription prices, and depth of coverage has linked the paper with a high-income and educated readership, drawing both positive and negative connotations.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In line with this, it claims to have influential readership of prominent business leaders and policy-makers.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>The Economist</i> was founded by the British businessman and banker <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(businessman)" title="James Wilson (businessman)">James Wilson</a> in 1843, to advance the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>, a system of import tariffs.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Prospectus_(finance)" title="Prospectus (finance)">prospectus</a> for the newspaper from 5 August 1843 enumerated thirteen areas of coverage that its editors wanted the publication to focus on:<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:155px;max-width:155px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_(IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1).pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_%28IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1%29.pdf/page1-153px-The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_%28IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1%29.pdf.jpg" decoding="async" width="153" height="246" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_%28IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1%29.pdf/page1-230px-The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_%28IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1%29.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_%28IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1%29.pdf/page1-306px-The_Economist_1843-09-02-_Vol_1_Iss_1_%28IA_sim_economist_1843-09-02_1_1%29.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1258" data-file-height="2020" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Scottish economist <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(businessman)" title="James Wilson (businessman)">James Wilson</a> founded the newspaper to "take part in a severe contest between intelligence." Its first issue was published on 2 September 1843 as a <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> newspaper before transitioning into a <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding#Thermally_activated_binding" title="Bookbinding">perfect-bound weekly</a> paper in 1971;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> the paper currently uses a <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding#Stitched_or_sewn_binding" title="Bookbinding">stapled magazine</a> format.</div></div></div></div>\n<ol><li>Original <a href="/wiki/Editorial" title="Editorial">leading articles</a>, in which free-trade principles will be most rigidly applied to all the important questions of the day.</li>\n<li>Articles relating to some practical, commercial, agricultural, or foreign topic of passing interest, such as foreign treaties.</li>\n<li>An article on the elementary principles of <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economy</a>, applied to practical experience, covering the laws related to prices, wages, rent, exchange, revenue and taxes.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliamentary</a> reports, with particular focus on commerce, agriculture and free trade.</li>\n<li>Reports and accounts of popular movements advocating free trade.</li>\n<li>General news from the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_St_James%27s" title="Court of St James&#39;s">Court of St James\'s</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">Metropolis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Historic_counties_of_England" title="Historic counties of England">Provinces</a>, Scotland, and Ireland.</li>\n<li>Commercial topics such as changes in fiscal regulations, the state and prospects of the markets, imports and exports, foreign news, the state of the manufacturing districts, notices of important new mechanical improvements, shipping news, the money market, and the progress of railways and public companies.</li>\n<li>Agricultural topics, including the application of geology and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>; notices of new and improved <a href="/wiki/List_of_agricultural_machinery" title="List of agricultural machinery">implements</a>, state of crops, markets, prices, foreign markets and prices converted into English money; from time to time, in some detail, the plans pursued in Belgium, Switzerland, and other well-cultivated countries.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/British_overseas_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="British overseas territories">Colonial</a> and foreign topics, including trade, produce, political and fiscal changes, and other matters, including <a href="/wiki/Investigative_journalism" title="Investigative journalism">expos\xc3\xa9s</a> on the evils of restriction and protection, and the advantages of free intercourse and trade.</li>\n<li>Law reports, confined chiefly to areas important to commerce, manufacturing, and agriculture.</li>\n<li>Books, confined chiefly, but not so exclusively, to commerce, manufacturing, and agriculture, and including all treatises on political economy, finance, or taxation.</li>\n<li>A commercial <a href="/wiki/Gazette" title="Gazette">gazette</a>, with prices and statistics of the week.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_editor" title="Letter to the editor">Correspondence and inquiries</a> from the newspaper\'s readers.</li></ol><p>\nWilson described it as taking part in "a severe contest between intelligence, which presses forward, and an unworthy, timid ignorance obstructing our progress", a phrase which still appears on its masthead as the publication\'s mission.<sup id="cite_ref-opinion_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-opinion-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> It has long been respected as "one of the most competent and subtle Western periodicals on public affairs".<sup id="cite_ref-Leites_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leites-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It was cited by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> in his formulation of socialist theory, because Marx felt the publication epitomised the interests of the bourgeoisie.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He wrote: "the London <i>Economist</i>, the European organ of the aristocracy of finance, described most strikingly the attitude of this class."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In 1915, revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> referred to <i>The Economist</i> as a "journal that speaks for British millionaires".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Additionally Lenin claimed that <i>The Economist</i> held a "bourgeois-pacifist" position and supported peace out of fear of <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg/220px-The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg/330px-The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg/440px-The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6240" data-file-height="4160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_2019_1_o.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A panel of journalists and public policy leaders at <i>The Economist</i>\'s 2019 India Summit.</div></div></div><p>In 1920, the paper\'s circulation rose to 6,170. In 1934, it underwent its first major redesign. The current <a href="/wiki/Fire_engine_red" title="Fire engine red">fire engine red</a> nameplate was created by <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_Stone" title="Reynolds Stone">Reynolds Stone</a> in 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-About_us_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_us-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In 1971, <i>The Economist</i> changed its <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet format</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding#Thermally_activated_binding" title="Bookbinding">magazine-style perfect-bound</a> formatting.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In January 2012, <i>The Economist</i> launched a new weekly section devoted exclusively to China, the first new country section since the introduction of one on the United States in 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1991, <a href="/wiki/James_Fallows" title="James Fallows">James Fallows</a> argued in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> that <i>The Economist</i> used editorial lines that contradicted the news stories they purported to highlight.<sup id="cite_ref-fallows_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fallows-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Sullivan" title="Andrew Sullivan">Andrew Sullivan</a> complained in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> that it uses "marketing genius"<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> to make up for deficiencies in original reporting, resulting in "a kind of <i><a href="/wiki/Reader%27s_Digest" title="Reader&#39;s Digest">Reader\'s Digest</a></i>"<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> for America\'s corporate elite.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> wrote that "its writers rarely see a political or economic problem that cannot be solved by the trusted three-card trick of privatisation, deregulation and liberalisation".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i> named it the best English-language paper noting its strength in international reporting where it does not feel moved to "cover a faraway land only at a time of unmitigated disaster" and that it kept a wall between its reporting and its more conservative editorial policies.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Meacham" title="Jon Meacham">Jon Meacham</a>, former editor of <i>Newsweek</i> and a self-described "fan", criticised <i>The Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> focus on analysis over original reporting.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, <i>The Economist</i> was <a href="/wiki/2012_ICT_Skype_controversy" title="2012 ICT Skype controversy">accused of hacking</a> into the computer of Justice <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Nizamul_Huq" title="Mohammed Nizamul Huq">Mohammed Nizamul Huq</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> Supreme Court, leading to his resignation as the chairman of the <a href="/wiki/International_Crimes_Tribunal_(Bangladesh)" title="International Crimes Tribunal (Bangladesh)">International Crimes Tribunal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ICTleaks_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ICTleaks1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICTleaks1-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In August 2015, <a href="/wiki/Pearson_plc" title="Pearson plc">Pearson</a> sold its 50% stake in the newspaper to the Italian <a href="/wiki/Agnelli_family" title="Agnelli family">Agnelli family</a>\'s investment company, <a href="/wiki/Exor_(company)" title="Exor (company)">Exor</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">\xc2\xa3</a>469 million (<a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>531 million) and the paper re-acquired the remaining shares for \xc2\xa3182 million ($206 million).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-West_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation">Organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shareholders">Shareholders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Shareholders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Economist_building_London1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Economist_building_London1.jpg/220px-Economist_building_London1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Economist_building_London1.jpg/330px-Economist_building_London1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Economist_building_London1.jpg/440px-Economist_building_London1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4239" data-file-height="5147" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Economist_building_London1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">City of Westminster</a>\'s Smithson Plaza, formerly known as The Economist Building,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> served as the headquarters of the paper until 2017, on <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Street" title="St James&#39;s Street">St James\'s Street</a>.</div></div></div><p><a href="/wiki/Pearson_plc" title="Pearson plc">Pearson plc</a> held a 50% shareholding via <a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">The Financial Times Limited</a> until August 2015. At that time, Pearson sold their share in the Economist. The <a href="/wiki/Agnelli_family" title="Agnelli family">Agnelli family</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Exor_(company)" title="Exor (company)">Exor</a> paid \xc2\xa3287m to raise their stake from 4.7% to 43.4% while the Economist paid \xc2\xa3182m for the balance of 5.04m shares which will be distributed to current shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-West_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Aside from the Agnelli family, smaller shareholders in the company include <a href="/wiki/Cadbury_family" title="Cadbury family">Cadbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rothschild_family" title="Rothschild family">Rothschild</a> (21%), <a href="/wiki/Schroders" title="Schroders">Schroder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baron_Layton" title="Baron Layton">Layton</a> and other family interests as well as a number of staff and former staff shareholders.<sup id="cite_ref-West_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-West-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Trustee" title="Trustee">board of trustees</a> formally appoints the editor, who cannot be removed without its permission. The Economist Newspaper Limited is a wholly owned subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/The_Economist_Group" title="The Economist Group">The Economist Group</a>. Sir <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Robert_de_Rothschild" class="mw-redirect" title="Evelyn Robert de Rothschild">Evelyn Robert de Rothschild</a> was Chairman of the company from 1972 to 1989.\n</p><p>Although <i>The Economist</i> has a global emphasis and scope, about two-thirds of the 75 staff journalists are based in the London borough of <a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">Westminster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> However, due to half of all subscribers originating in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <i>The Economist</i> has core editorial offices and substantial operations in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington D.C.</a><sup id="cite_ref-gainsreaders_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gainsreaders-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Editor">Editor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Editor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:256px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg/254px-Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="254" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg/381px-Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg/508px-Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2133" data-file-height="1333" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zanny_Minton_Beddoes_World_Economic_Forum_2013_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes" title="Zanny Minton Beddoes">Zanny Minton Beddoes</a> was appointed editor in 2015, first joining as an <a href="/wiki/Emerging_markets" class="mw-redirect" title="Emerging markets">emerging markets</a> correspondent in 1994.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Editor-in-chief" title="Editor-in-chief">editor-in-chief</a>, commonly known simply as "the Editor", of <i>The Economist</i> is charged with formulating the paper\'s editorial policies and overseeing corporate operations. Since its 1843 founding, the editors have been:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/James_Wilson_(businessman)" title="James Wilson (businessman)">James Wilson</a>: 1843\xe2\x80\x931857</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Holt_Hutton" title="Richard Holt Hutton">Richard Holt Hutton</a>: 1857\xe2\x80\x931861<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a>: 1861\xe2\x80\x931877<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Conner_Lathbury" title="Daniel Conner Lathbury">Daniel Conner Lathbury</a>: 1877\xe2\x80\x931881<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> (<i>jointly</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Harry_Inglis_Palgrave" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Harry Inglis Palgrave">Robert Harry Inglis Palgrave</a>: 1877\xe2\x80\x931883 (<i>jointly</i>)</li>\n<li>Edward Johnstone: 1883\xe2\x80\x931907<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Francis_Wrigley_Hirst" class="mw-redirect" title="Francis Wrigley Hirst">Francis Wrigley Hirst</a>: 1907\xe2\x80\x931916</li>\n<li>Hartley Withers: 1916\xe2\x80\x931921</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Walter_Layton,_1st_Baron_Layton" title="Walter Layton, 1st Baron Layton">Sir Walter Layton</a>: 1922\xe2\x80\x931938</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Crowther,_Baron_Crowther" title="Geoffrey Crowther, Baron Crowther">Geoffrey Crowther</a>: 1938\xe2\x80\x931956</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Donald_Tyerman" title="Donald Tyerman">Donald Tyerman</a>: 1956\xe2\x80\x931965</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Alastair_Burnet" title="Alastair Burnet">Sir Alastair Burnet</a>: 1965\xe2\x80\x931974</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Knight_(journalist)" title="Andrew Knight (journalist)">Andrew Knight</a>: 1974\xe2\x80\x931986</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rupert_Pennant-Rea" title="Rupert Pennant-Rea">Rupert Pennant-Rea</a>: 1986\xe2\x80\x931993</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bill_Emmott" title="Bill Emmott">Bill Emmott</a>: 1993\xe2\x80\x932006</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Micklethwait" title="John Micklethwait">John Micklethwait</a>: 2006\xe2\x80\x932014<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes" title="Zanny Minton Beddoes">Zanny Minton Beddoes</a>: 2015\xe2\x80\x93present<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tone_and_voice">Tone and voice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tone and voice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Though it has many individual columns, by tradition and current practice the newspaper ensures a uniform voice\xe2\x80\x94aided by the anonymity of writers\xe2\x80\x94throughout its pages,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> as if most articles were written by a single author, which may be perceived to display dry, understated wit, and precise use of language.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s treatment of economics presumes a working familiarity with fundamental concepts of classical economics. For instance, it does not explain terms like <a href="/wiki/Invisible_hand" title="Invisible hand">invisible hand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macroeconomics" title="Macroeconomics">macroeconomics</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Demand_curve" title="Demand curve">demand curve</a>, and may take just six or seven words to explain the theory of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">comparative advantage</a>. Articles involving economics do not presume any formal training on the part of the reader and aim to be accessible to the educated layman. It usually does not translate short French (and German) quotes or phrases. It does describe the business or nature of even well-known entities, writing, for example, "<a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>, an investment bank".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> is known for its extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Word_play" title="Word play">word play</a>, including puns, allusions, and metaphors, as well as alliteration and assonance, especially in its headlines and captions. This can make it difficult to understand for those who are not native English speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Economist</i> has traditionally and historically persisted in referring to itself as a "<a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspaper</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> rather than a "<a href="/wiki/News_magazine" title="News magazine">news magazine</a>" due to its mostly cosmetic switch from <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bookbinding#Thermally_activated_binding" title="Bookbinding">perfect-binding</a> format and its general focus on <a href="/wiki/Current_affairs_(news_format)" title="Current affairs (news format)">current affairs</a> as opposed to specialist subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pgazette_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pgazette-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> It is <a href="/wiki/Newspaper_Libel_and_Registration_Act_1881" title="Newspaper Libel and Registration Act 1881">legally classified as a newspaper in Britain</a> and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Most databases and <a href="/wiki/Anthology" title="Anthology">anthologies</a> catalogue the weekly as a newspaper printed in magazine- or journal-format.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> differentiates and contrasts itself as a newspaper against their sister lifestyle magazine, <i><a href="/wiki/1843_(magazine)" title="1843 (magazine)">1843</a></i>, which does the same in turn. Editor <a href="/wiki/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes" title="Zanny Minton Beddoes">Zanny Minton Bedoes</a> clarified the distinction in 2016: "we call it a newspaper because it was founded in 1843, 173 years ago, [when] all [perfect-bound publications] were called newspapers."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Editorial_anonymity">Editorial anonymity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Editorial anonymity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:160px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_Article.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_Economist_Article.jpg/158px-The_Economist_Article.jpg" decoding="async" width="158" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_Economist_Article.jpg/238px-The_Economist_Article.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_Economist_Article.jpg/316px-The_Economist_Article.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_Article.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Economist</i> articles typically do not carry <a href="/wiki/Byline" title="Byline">bylines</a>, anonymously publishing their work.</div></div></div>\n<p>Articles often take a definite editorial stance and almost never carry a <a href="/wiki/Byline" title="Byline">byline</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Not even the name of the editor is printed in the issue. It is a long-standing tradition that an editor\'s only signed article during their tenure is written on the occasion of their departure from the position. The author of a piece is named in certain circumstances: when notable persons are invited to contribute opinion pieces; when journalists of <i>The Economist</i> compile special reports (previously known as surveys); for the Year in Review special edition; and to highlight a potential <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a> over a book review. The names of <i>The Economist</i> editors and correspondents can be located on the media directory pages of the website.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Online blog pieces are signed with the initials of the writer and authors of print stories are allowed to note their authorship from their personal web sites.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> "This approach is not without its faults (we have four staff members with the initials \'J.P.\', for example) but is the best compromise between total anonymity and full bylines, in our view", wrote one anonymous writer of <i>The Economist</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> There are three editorial and business areas in which the anonymous ethos of the weekly has contributed to strengthening its unique identity: collective and consistent voice, talent and newsroom management, and brand strength and clarity.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The editors say this is necessary because "collective voice and personality matter more than the identities of individual journalists"<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and reflects "a collaborative effort".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In most articles, authors refer to themselves as "your correspondent" or "this reviewer". The writers of the titled opinion columns tend to refer to themselves by the title (hence, a sentence in the "Lexington" column might read "Lexington was informed...").\n</p><p>American author and long-time reader <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis" title="Michael Lewis">Michael Lewis</a> criticised the paper\'s editorial anonymity in 1991, labelling it a means to hide the youth and inexperience of those writing articles.<sup id="cite_ref-fallows_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fallows-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Although individual articles are written anonymously, there is no secrecy over who the writers are, as they are listed on <i>The Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> website, which also provides summaries of their careers and academic qualifications.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Later, in 2009, Lewis included multiple <i>Economist</i> articles in his <a href="/wiki/Anthology" title="Anthology">anthology</a> about the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x9308">2008 financial crisis</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Panic:_The_Story_of_Modern_Financial_Insanity" title="Panic: The Story of Modern Financial Insanity">Panic: The Story of Modern Financial Insanity</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Ralston_Saul" title="John Ralston Saul">John Ralston Saul</a> describes <i>The Economist</i> as a "...[newspaper] which hides the names of the journalists who write its articles in order to create the illusion that they dispense disinterested truth rather than opinion. This sales technique, reminiscent of pre-Reformation Catholicism, is not surprising in a publication named after the social science most given to wild guesses and imaginary facts presented in the guise of inevitability and exactitude. That it is the Bible of the corporate executive indicates to what extent received wisdom is the daily bread of a managerial civilization."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg/207px-A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg" decoding="async" width="207" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg/310px-A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg/414px-A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1512" data-file-height="2016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_stack_of_Economist_papers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stack of <i>Economist</i> papers, ordered by publication date, 2020.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s primary focus is world events, politics and business, but it also runs regular sections on science and technology as well as books and the arts. Approximately every two weeks, the publication includes an in-depth special report (previously called <a href="/wiki/Survey_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Survey article">surveys</a>) on a given topic.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The five main categories are Countries and Regions, Business, Finance and Economics, Science, and Technology. The newspaper goes to press on Thursdays, between 6&#160;pm and 7&#160;pm GMT, and is available at newsagents in many countries the next day. It is printed at seven sites around the world.\n</p><p>Since July 2007, there has also been a complete audio edition of the paper available 9&#160;pm London time on Thursdays.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The audio version of <i>The Economist</i> is produced by the production company <i>Talking Issues</i>. The company records the full text of the newspaper in <a href="/wiki/MP3" title="MP3">MP3</a> format, including the extra pages in the UK edition. The weekly 130 MB download is free for subscribers and available for a fee for non-subscribers. The publication\'s writers adopt a tight style that seeks to include the maximum amount of information in a limited space.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_G._Bradley" title="David G. Bradley">David G. Bradley</a>, publisher of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i>, described the formula as "a consistent world view expressed, consistently, in tight and engaging prose".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">Letters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Letters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>The Economist</i> frequently receives letters from its readership in response to the previous week\'s edition. While it is known to feature letters from senior businesspeople, politicians, ambassadors, and spokespeople, the paper includes letters from typical readers as well. Well-written or witty responses from anyone are considered, and controversial issues frequently produce a torrent of letters. For example, the survey of <a href="/wiki/Corporate_social_responsibility" title="Corporate social responsibility">corporate social responsibility</a>, published January 2005, produced largely critical letters from <a href="/wiki/Oxfam" title="Oxfam">Oxfam</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Global_Compact" title="United Nations Global Compact">United Nations Global Compact</a>, the Chairman of <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">BT Group</a>, an ex-Director of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Shell</a> and the UK <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Directors" title="Institute of Directors">Institute of Directors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an effort to foster diversity of thought, <i>The Economist</i> routinely publishes letters that openly criticize the paper\'s articles and stance. After <i>The Economist</i> ran a critique of <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> and human rights in general in its issue dated 24 March 2007, its letters page ran a reply from Amnesty, as well as several other letters in support of the organisation, including one from the head of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Commission on Human Rights">United Nations Commission on Human Rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Rebuttals from officials within regimes such as the Singapore government are routinely printed, to comply with local <a href="/wiki/Right-of-reply" class="mw-redirect" title="Right-of-reply">right-of-reply</a> laws without compromising editorial independence.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Letters published in the paper are typically between 150 and 200 words long and had the now-discontinued salutation \'Sir\' from 1843 to 2015. In the latter year, upon the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Zanny_Minton_Beddoes" title="Zanny Minton Beddoes">Zanny Minton Beddoes</a>, the first female editor, the salutation was dismissed; letters have since had no salutation. Previous to a change in procedure, all responses to online articles were usually published in "The Inbox".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columns">Columns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Columns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:225px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Economist_Cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Economist_Cartoon.jpg/223px-Economist_Cartoon.jpg" decoding="async" width="223" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Economist_Cartoon.jpg/335px-Economist_Cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Economist_Cartoon.jpg/446px-Economist_Cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Economist_Cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Political_cartoon" title="Political cartoon">political cartoon</a> published by the newspaper in November 2010, depicting  the <a href="/wiki/European_debt_crisis" title="European debt crisis">2010 European sovereign debt crisis</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The publication runs several opinion columns whose names reflect their topic:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><b>Babbage</b></i> (Technology): named for the inventor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">Charles Babbage</a>, this column was established in March 2010 and focuses on various technology related issues.</li>\n<li><i><b>Bagehot</b></i> (Britain): named for <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="/d\xca\x92/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">d\xca\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>), 19th-century British constitutional expert and early editor of <i>The Economist</i>. Since April 2017 it has been written by <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Wooldridge" title="Adrian Wooldridge">Adrian Wooldridge</a>, who succeeded <a href="/wiki/David_Rennie_(columnist)" title="David Rennie (columnist)">David Rennie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><b>Banyan</b></i> (Asia): named for the <a href="/wiki/Banyan" title="Banyan">banyan</a> tree, this column was established in April 2009 and focuses on various issues across the Asian continent, and is written by <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Ziegler" title="Dominic Ziegler">Dominic Ziegler</a>.</li>\n<li><i><b>Baobab</b></i> (Africa &amp; Middle East): named for the <a href="/wiki/Baobab" class="mw-redirect" title="Baobab">baobab</a> tree, this column was established in July 2010 and focuses on various issues across the African continent.</li>\n<li><i><b>Bartleby</b></i> (Work and management): named after <a href="/wiki/Bartleby" class="mw-redirect" title="Bartleby">the titular character</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a> short story, this column was established in May 2018. It is written by <a href="/wiki/Philip_Coggan" title="Philip Coggan">Philip Coggan</a>.</li>\n<li><i><b>Bello</b></i> (Latin America): named for <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Bello" title="Andr\xc3\xa9s Bello">Andr\xc3\xa9s Bello</a>, a Venezuelan diplomat, poet, legislator and philosopher, who lived and worked in Chile.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The column was established in January 2014 and is written by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Reid_(journalist_and_writer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Reid (journalist and writer)">Michael Reid</a>.</li>\n<li><i><b>Buttonwood</b></i> (Finance): named for the <a href="/wiki/Platanus_occidentalis" title="Platanus occidentalis">buttonwood</a> tree where early <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> traders gathered. Until September 2006 this was available only as an on-line column, but it is now included in the print edition. Since 2018, it is written by John O\'Sullivan, succeeding Philip Coggan.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><b>Chaguan</b></i> (China): named for Chaguan, the traditional Chinese Tea houses in <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a>, this column was established on 13 September 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><b>Charlemagne</b></i> (Europe): named for <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, Emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Frankish Empire</a>. It is written by Jeremy Cliffe<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and earlier it was written by David Rennie (2007\xe2\x80\x932010) and by Anton La Guardia<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> (2010\xe2\x80\x932014).</li>\n<li><i><b>Erasmus</b></i> (Religion and public policy) \xe2\x80\x93 named after the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Christian_humanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian humanist">Christian humanist</a> <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>.</li>\n<li><i><b>Game Theory</b></i> (Sport): named after <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">the science of predicting outcomes</a> in a certain situation, this column focuses on "sports major and minor" and "the politics, economics, science and statistics of the games we play and watch".</li>\n<li><i><b>Johnson</b></i> (language): named for <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, this column returned to the publication in 2016 and covers language. It is written by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Lane_Greene" title="Robert Lane Greene">Robert Lane Greene</a>.</li>\n<li><i><b>Lexington</b></i> (United States): named for <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Massachusetts" title="Lexington, Massachusetts">Lexington, Massachusetts</a>, the site of the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. From June 2010 until May 2012 it was written by <a href="/wiki/Peter_David_(journalist)" title="Peter David (journalist)">Peter David</a>, until his death in a car accident.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><b>Prospero</b></i> (Books and arts): named after the character from <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="William Shakespeare&#39;s">William Shakespeare\'s</a> play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">The Tempest</a></i>, this column reviews books and focuses on arts-related issues.</li>\n<li><i><b>Schumpeter</b></i> (Business): named for the economist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a>, this column was established in September 2009 and is written by Patrick Foulis.</li>\n<li><i><b>Free Exchange</b></i> (Economics): a general economics column, frequently based on academic research, replaced the column <i>Economics Focus</i> in January 2012</li>\n<li><b>Obituary</b> (recent death): Since 1997 it has been written by <a href="/wiki/Ann_Wroe" title="Ann Wroe">Ann Wroe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="TQ"><i>TQ</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: TQ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Every three months, <i>The Economist</i> publishes a <a href="/wiki/Technology_journalism" title="Technology journalism">technology report</a> called <i>Technology Quarterly</i>, or simply, <i>TQ</i>, a special section focusing on recent trends and developments in science and technology.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The feature is also known to intertwine "economic matters with a technology".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The <i>TQ</i> often carries a theme, such as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_computing" title="Quantum computing">quantum computing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cloud_storage" title="Cloud storage">cloud storage</a>, and assembles an assortment of articles around the common subject.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1843"><i>1843</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1843">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1843_(magazine)" title="1843 (magazine)">1843 (magazine)</a></div>\n<p>In September 2007, <i>The Economist</i> newspaper launched a sister <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_magazine" title="Lifestyle magazine">lifestyle magazine</a> under the title <i>Intelligent Life</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Magazine" title="Magazine">quarterly publication</a>. At its inguaration it was billed as for "the arts, style, food, wine, cars, travel and anything else under the sun, as long as it\xe2\x80\x99s interesting".<sup id="cite_ref-faqs_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faqs-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The magazine focuses on analyzing the "insights and predictions for the <a href="/wiki/Luxury_goods" title="Luxury goods">luxury landscape</a>" across the world.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Approximately ten years later, in March 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Economist_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Economist Group">newspaper\'s parent company</a> rebranded the lifestyle magazine as <i>1843</i>, in honor of the paper\'s founding year. It has since remained at six issues per year and carries the motto "Stories of An Extraordinary World."<sup id="cite_ref-faqs_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faqs-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Unlike <i>The Economist</i>, the author\'s names appear next to their articles in <i>1843.</i><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>1843</i> features contributions from <i>Economist</i> journalists as well as writers around the world and photography commissioned for each issue. It is seen as a market competitor to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/WSJ." title="WSJ.">WSJ.</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i>\' <i><a href="/wiki/FT_Magazine" title="FT Magazine">FT Magazine</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> It has, since its March 2016 relaunch, been edited by Rosie Blau, a former correspondent for <i>The Economist</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_World_Ahead"><i>The World Ahead</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The World Ahead">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The paper also produces two annual reviews and predicative reports titled <i>The World In [Year]</i> and <i>The World If [Year]</i> as part of their <i>The World Ahead</i> franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In both features, the newspaper publishes a review of the social, cultural, economic and political events that have shaped the year and will continue to influence the immediate future. The issue was described by the American think tank <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> as "<i>The Economist</i>\'s annual [150-page] exercise in <a href="/wiki/Forecasting" title="Forecasting">forecasting</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An Urdu-language version of <i>The World In [Year]</i> in collaboration with <i>The Economist</i> is being distributed by <a href="/wiki/Jang_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Jang Group">Jang Group</a> in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:280px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_Books.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Economist_Books.jpg/278px-The_Economist_Books.jpg" decoding="async" width="278" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Economist_Books.jpg/417px-The_Economist_Books.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Economist_Books.jpg/556px-The_Economist_Books.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3963" data-file-height="2025" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Economist_Books.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A series of <i>Economist</i> <a href="/wiki/Technical_communication" title="Technical communication">technical manuals</a>, 2020</div></div></div>\n<p>In addition to publishing its main newspaper, <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_magazine" title="Lifestyle magazine">lifestyle magazine</a>, and special features, <i>The Economist</i> also produces books with topics overlapping with that of its newspaper. The weekly also publishes a series of <a href="/wiki/Technical_communication" title="Technical communication">technical manuals</a> (or guides) as an offshoot of its <a href="/wiki/Analytic_journalism" title="Analytic journalism">explanatory journalism</a>. Some of these books serve as collections of articles and columns the paper produces.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Often columnists from the newspaper write technical manuals on their topic of expertise; for example, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Coggan" title="Philip Coggan">Philip Coggan</a>, a finance correspondent, authored <i>The Economist Guide to Hedge Funds</i> (2011).<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Additionally, the paper publishes <a href="/wiki/Book_review" title="Book review">book reviews</a> in every issue, with a large collective review in their year-end (holiday) issue \xe2\x80\x93 published as "<i>The Economist</i>\'s Books of the Year".<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The paper has its own <a href="/wiki/List_of_style_guides" title="List of style guides">in-house stylebook</a> rather than following an industry-wide writing style template.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> All <i>Economist</i> writing and publications follow <i>The Economist Style Guide</i>, in various editions.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_competitions">Writing competitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Writing competitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>The Economist</i> sponsors a wide-array of writing competitions and prices throughout the year for readers. In 1999, <i>The Economist</i> organised a global futurist writing competition, <a href="/wiki/The_World_in_2050" title="The World in 2050">The World in 2050</a>. Co-sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch/Shell" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Dutch/Shell">Royal Dutch/Shell</a>, the competition included a first prize of <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>20,000 and publication in <i>The Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s annual flagship publication, <i>The World In</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-newstraightstimes2000_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newstraightstimes2000-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Over 3,000 entries from around the world were submitted via a website set up for the purpose and at various Royal Dutch Shell offices worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-newstraightstimes2000_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newstraightstimes2000-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The judging panel included <a href="/wiki/Bill_Emmott" title="Bill Emmott">Bill Emmott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esther_Dyson" title="Esther Dyson">Esther Dyson</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Mark_Moody-Stuart" title="Mark Moody-Stuart">Mark Moody-Stuart</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Matt_Ridley" title="Matt Ridley">Matt Ridley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the summer of 2019, they launched the Open Future writing competition with an inaugural youth essay-writing prompt about <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> During this competition the paper accepted a submission from an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificially-intelligent</a> computer writing program.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_journalism">Data journalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Data journalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:196px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg/194px-A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg" decoding="async" width="194" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg/292px-A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg/388px-A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1512" data-file-height="2016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_graphic_detail_of_The_Economist.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The paper launched a large <a href="/wiki/Data_journalism" title="Data journalism">data journalism</a> platform in 2015.</div></div></div>\n<p>The presence of <a href="/wiki/Data_journalism" title="Data journalism">data journalism</a> in <i>The Economist</i> can be traced to its founding year in 1843. Initially, the weekly published basic <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> figures and tables.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The paper first included a graphical model in 1847, with a <a href="/wiki/Bubble_chart" title="Bubble chart">bubble chart</a> detailing <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a>, and its first <a href="/wiki/Epistolary_novel" title="Epistolary novel">non-epistolary chart</a> was included in its 1854 issue, charting the <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">spread of cholera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This early adoption of data-based articles was estimated to be "a 100 years before <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">the field\xe2\x80\x99s modern emergence</a>" by <i>Data Journalism.com.</i><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Its transition from <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> to <a href="/wiki/Magazine" title="Magazine">magazine</a>-style formatting led to the adoption of colored graphs, first in <a href="/wiki/Fire_engine_red" title="Fire engine red">fire-engine-red</a> during the 1980s and then to a thematic blue in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Economist</i> told their readers throughout the 2000s that the paper\'s editors had "developed a taste for data-driven stories".<sup id="cite_ref-:02_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Starting in the late-2000s, they began to publish more and more articles that centered solely on charts, some of which began to be published daily.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The daily charts are typically followed by a short, 300-word explanation. In September 2009, <i>The Economist</i> launched a <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> account for their Data Team.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015, the weekly formed a dedicated team of 12 <a href="/wiki/Data_analysis" title="Data analysis">data analysts</a>, designers, and journalists to head up their firm-wide data journalism efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In order to ensure transparency in their data collection <i>The Economist</i> maintains a corporate <a href="/wiki/GitHub" title="GitHub">GitHub</a> account to publicly disclose all of their models and software.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In October 2018, they introduced their "Graphic Detail" feature in both their print and digital editions.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Graphic Detail feature would go on to include mainly graphs, maps, and <a href="/wiki/Infographic" title="Infographic">infographics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Economist</i>\'s Data Team won the 2020 Sigma Data Journalism Award for Best Young Journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, they placed third for an infographic describing <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Israel" title="Politics of Israel">Israel\'s coalition networks</a> in the year\'s Data Journalism Awards by the <a href="/wiki/Global_Editors_Network" title="Global Editors Network">Global Editors Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indexes">Indexes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Indexes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Historically, the publication has also maintained a section of <a href="/wiki/Economic_statistics" title="Economic statistics">economic statistics</a>, such as employment figures, economic growth, and interest rates. These statistical publications have been found to be seen as authoritative and decisive in British society.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> also publishes a variety of rankings seeking to position <a href="/wiki/Business_school" title="Business school">business schools</a> and <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_education" title="Undergraduate education">undergraduate universities</a> among each other, respectively. In 2015, they published their first ranking of U.S. universities, focusing on comparable economical advantages. Their data for the rankings is sourced from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Education" title="United States Department of Education">U.S. Department of Education</a> and is calculated as a function of median earnings through <a href="/wiki/Regression_analysis" title="Regression analysis">regression analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Among others, the most well-known <a href="/wiki/Index_(economics)" title="Index (economics)">data indexes</a> the weekly publishes are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Big_Mac_Index" title="Big Mac Index">Big Mac Index</a>: a measure of the <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power</a> of currencies, first published in 1986, using the price of the hamburger in different countries.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> This is published twice a year, annually.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">Democracy Index</a>: a measure of the state of democracy in the world, produced by the paper\'s <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">Economist Intelligence Unit</a> (EIU)</li>\n<li>The Glass Ceiling Index: a measure of <a href="/wiki/Occupational_sexism" title="Occupational sexism">female equality in the workplace</a>.</li>\n<li>The Most Dangerous Cities Index: a measure of major cities <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_intentional_homicide_rate" title="List of countries by intentional homicide rate">by rates of homicide</a>.</li>\n<li>Commodity-Price Index: a measure of <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brent_Crude" title="Brent Crude">brent oil</a>, as well as agricultural items</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opinions">Opinions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Economist&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Opinions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Economist_editorial_stance" title="The Economist editorial stance">The Economist editorial stance</a></div>\n<p>The editorial stance of <i>The Economist</i> primarily revolves around <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">social</a>, and most notably, <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>. Since its founding, it has supported <a href="/wiki/Radical_centrism" title="Radical centrism">radical centrism</a>, favouring policies and governments that maintain <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrist politics</a>. The newspaper typically champions <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_immigration" class="mw-redirect" title="Free immigration">free immigration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Globalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> When the newspaper was founded, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Economism" title="Economism">economism</a></i> denoted what would today be termed "economic liberalism". The activist and journalist <a href="/wiki/George_Monbiot" title="George Monbiot">George Monbiot</a> has described it as neoliberal while occasionally accepting the propositions of <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a> where deemed more "reasonable".<sup id="cite_ref-Grauniad_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grauniad-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The weekly favours a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> to fight <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> According to one former editor, Bill Emmott, "the <i>Economist</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s philosophy has always been liberal, not conservative".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues,_Edinburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues%2C_Edinburgh.jpg/220px-David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues%2C_Edinburgh.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues%2C_Edinburgh.jpg/330px-David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues%2C_Edinburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues%2C_Edinburgh.jpg/440px-David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues%2C_Edinburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1898" data-file-height="2496" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Hume_and_Adam_Smith_statues,_Edinburgh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scottish economist <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> (right) and philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> (left) represent the newspaper\'s foundational beliefs of <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a></i> policies, self-sufficiency, anti-protectionism and <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Individual contributors take diverse views. <i>The Economist</i> favours the support, through <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central banks</a>, of banks and other important corporations. This principle can, in a much more limited form, be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a>, the third editor of <i>The Economist</i>, who argued that the Bank of England should support major banks that got into difficulties. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> deemed <i>The Economist</i> the "European organ" of "the aristocracy of finance".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The newspaper has also supported liberal causes on social issues such as recognition of gay marriages,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> legalisation of drugs,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> criticises the <a href="/wiki/Taxation_in_the_United_States" title="Taxation in the United States">US tax model</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and seems to support some government regulation on health issues, such as smoking in public,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> as well as bans on spanking children.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> consistently favours guest worker programmes, <a href="/wiki/School_choice" title="School choice">parental choice of school</a>, and amnesties<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and once published an "obituary" of God.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Economist</i> also has a long record of supporting gun control.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Economist</i> has endorsed the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> (in 2005), the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> (in 2010 and 2015),<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Economist_137-0" class="reference"><a
